Boolean functions arise in many areas of computer science and mathematics, e.g., in circuit design,formal logics, coding theory, artificial intelligence, machine learning, and system analysis crama_hammer_2011; o2014analysis.When modeling and analyzing systems through Boolean functions, many design decisions areaffected by the relevance of variables for the outcome of the function.Examples include noise-reduction components for important input variables to increasereliability of circuits, prioritizing important variables in decision-making of protocols,or the order of variables in BDDs bryantSymbolicBooleanManipulation1992; bartlettComparisonTwoNew2001.Many ideas to quantify such notions of importance of variables in Boolean functionshave since been considered in the literature.To mention a few, influence ben-orCollectiveCoinFlipping1985is used to determine power of actors in voting schemes, hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000devised measures based on how constant a function becomes depending on variable assignments,blame chocklerResponsibilityBlameStructuralModel2004 quantifies the average responsibility chocklerWhatCausesSystem2008of input variables on the outcome of circuits or on causalreasoning, andthe Jeroslow-Wang value jeroslowSolvingPropositionalSatisfiability1990 quantifiesimportance of variables in CNFs to derive splitting rules for SAT-solvers hookerBranchingRulesSatisfiability1995.Closely related are notions of impact in cooperative games, e.g.,through the Shapley value shapleyValueNPersonGames1953or the Banzhaf value banzhafWeightedVotingDoesn1965.

Although some of the aforementioned concepts are of quite different natureand serve different purposes, they share some common ideas.This raises the question of what characteristics importance valueshave and how the notions of the literature relate.The motivation of this paper is to advance the understanding of importance values, independentof concrete applications. For this purpose, we introduce a genericaxiomatic framework that constitutes the classof importance value functions (IVFs).Our axioms are motivated by properties one would intuitively expect from IVFs, e.g.,that independent variables have no importance or that permutations do not changeimportance values. We show basic relationships within and between IVFs and provide new insightsfor existing and new importance measures.By connecting Boolean functions and cooperative games through cooperative game mappings (CGMs) and using Shapley and Banzhaf values,we show how to generically derive new IVFs .All aforementioned notions of importance values from the literature satisfy our IVF axioms, showing that we provide a unifying frameworkfor all these notions, including CGM-derived ones.

Most notions of importance are known to be computationally hard, e.g., computinginfluence or the Shapley value is #P-complete traxler2009variable; FaiKer92; DenPap94.We address computational aspects by devising practical computation schemes for IVFs usingprojected model counting aziz2015exists and BDDs.

In summary, our main contribution is an axiomatic definition of IVFs for variables in Boolean functions (Section 3),covering notions of importance from the literature (Sections 4.1 and 4.2).Moreover, we derive novel IVFs by linking Boolean functions with cooperative games and related values (Section 4.3).Finally, we provide practical computation schemes for IVFs (Section 5).

This is a preprint of the paper accepted at the 32nd International Joint Conferences on Artificial Intelligence (IJCAI’23). It includes proofs and other additional material in the appendix.An implementation of the computing schemes for IVFs can be found at https://github.com/graps1/impmeas.

{toappendix}

There are a few properties of the derivative operator which are useful to point out. First, taking the derivative with respect to a function that does not depend on x𝑥x results in the constant zero function (2.5). Second, it is multiplicative in the sense that functions which do not depend on x𝑥x can be moved in front of the operator (2.6). And third, it is additive in the sense of (2.7):

{apxlemma}Let f,h,g𝑓ℎ𝑔f,h,g be Boolean functions such that x∉𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝑥𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔x\not\in\mathtt{dep}(g). ThenDx​g=0subscriptD𝑥𝑔0\displaystyle\mathrm{D}_{x}g=0(2.5)Dx​(g​f)=g​Dx​fsubscriptD𝑥𝑔𝑓𝑔subscriptD𝑥𝑓\displaystyle\mathrm{D}_{x}(gf)=g\mathrm{D}_{x}f(2.6)Dx​(f⊕h)=Dx​f⊕Dx​h.subscriptD𝑥direct-sum𝑓ℎdirect-sumsubscriptD𝑥𝑓subscriptD𝑥ℎ\displaystyle\mathrm{D}_{x}(f\oplus h)=\mathrm{D}_{x}f\oplus\mathrm{D}_{x}h.(2.7)

(2.5) follows from the fact that gx/1=gx/0subscript𝑔𝑥1subscript𝑔𝑥0g_{x{/}1}=g_{x{/}0}. For (2.6), we get (g​f)x/1⊕(g​f)x/0=g​(fx/1⊕fx/0)direct-sumsubscript𝑔𝑓𝑥1subscript𝑔𝑓𝑥0𝑔direct-sumsubscript𝑓𝑥1subscript𝑓𝑥0(gf)_{x{/}1}\oplus(gf)_{x{/}0}=g(f_{x{/}1}\oplus f_{x{/}0}) since gx/1=gx/0=gsubscript𝑔𝑥1subscript𝑔𝑥0𝑔g_{x{/}1}=g_{x{/}0}=g. Expectations are frequently used throughout this paper, so let us establish a few simple equalities. First, one can compute the expected value of a pseudo Boolean function with respect to every superset of its dependent variables. Second, if two pseudo Boolean functions depend on mutually exclusive variables (dependence defined as for Boolean functions), then the expectation of their product can be decomposed. And third, expected values of modular Boolean functions admit decompositions as well:

{apxlemma}(i)If f𝑓f is a pseudo Boolean function and 𝚍𝚎𝚙​(f)⊆R𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑓𝑅\mathtt{dep}(f)\subseteq R, then 𝔼𝒖∈{0,1}X​[f​(𝒖)]=𝔼𝒖∈{0,1}R​[f​(𝒖)]subscript𝔼𝒖superscript01𝑋delimited-[]𝑓𝒖subscript𝔼𝒖superscript01𝑅delimited-[]𝑓𝒖\mathbb{E}_{{\bm{u}}\in\{0,1\}^{X}}[f({\bm{u}})]=\mathbb{E}_{{\bm{u}}\in\{0,1\}^{R}}[f({\bm{u}})].(ii)If f,g𝑓𝑔f,g are pseudo Boolean functions such that 𝚍𝚎𝚙​(f)∩𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)=∅𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑓𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔\mathtt{dep}(f)\cap\mathtt{dep}(g)=\varnothing, then 𝔼​[f⋅g]=𝔼​[f]⋅𝔼​[g]𝔼delimited-[]⋅𝑓𝑔⋅𝔼delimited-[]𝑓𝔼delimited-[]𝑔\mathbb{E}[f\cdot g]=\mathbb{E}[f]\cdot\mathbb{E}[g].(iii)If f,g𝑓𝑔f,g are Boolean functions such that f𝑓f is modular in g𝑔g, then𝔼​[f]=𝔼​[g]​𝔼​[fg/1]+(1−𝔼​[g])​𝔼​[fg/0].𝔼delimited-[]𝑓𝔼delimited-[]𝑔𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝑓𝑔11𝔼delimited-[]𝑔𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝑓𝑔0\mathbb{E}[f]=\mathbb{E}[g]\mathbb{E}[f_{g{/}1}]+(1-\mathbb{E}[g])\mathbb{E}[f_{g{/}0}].

We introduce a notion of modularity to capture independence of subfunctionsas common in the theory of Boolean functions and relatedfields ashenhurstDecompositionSwitchingFunctions1957; birnbaumModulesCoherentBinary1965; shapleyCompoundSimpleGames1967; biochDecompositionBooleanFunctions2010. Intuitively, f𝑓f is modular in g𝑔g if f𝑓f treats g𝑔g like a subfunction and otherwise ignores all variables that g𝑔g depends on.We define modularity in terms of a template function ℓℓ\ellin which g𝑔g is represented by a variable x𝑥x:

We frequently rely on the derivative of a Boolean function f𝑓f w.r.t. variable x𝑥x,Dx​f=fx/1⊕fx/0,subscriptD𝑥𝑓direct-sumsubscript𝑓𝑥1subscript𝑓𝑥0\mathrm{D}_{x}f=f_{x{/}1}\oplus f_{x{/}0},which encodes the undirected change of f𝑓f w.r.t. x𝑥x.For example, f=x∨y𝑓𝑥𝑦f=x\lor y has the derivative Dx​f=y¯subscriptD𝑥𝑓¯𝑦\mathrm{D}_{x}f=\overline{y}, with the intuition that x𝑥x can only have an impact if y𝑦y is set to zero.Furthermore, if f𝑓f is modular in g𝑔g, we define the derivative of f𝑓f w.r.t. g𝑔g as Dg​f=fg/1⊕fg/0subscriptD𝑔𝑓direct-sumsubscript𝑓𝑔1subscript𝑓𝑔0\mathrm{D}_{g}f=f_{g{/}1}\oplus f_{g{/}0}.Given this, we obtain the following lemma corresponding to the chain rule known in calculus:

{toappendix}

In this section, we devise axiomatic properties that should be fulfilled byevery reasonable importance attribution scheme.

For a Boolean function f𝑓f and a variable x𝑥x, we quantify the importanceof x𝑥x in f𝑓f by a number ℑx​(f)∈ℝsubscriptℑ𝑥𝑓ℝ\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f)\in\mathbb{R}, computed by some value function ℑℑ\mathfrak{I}.Not every value makes intutive sense when interpreted as the “importance”of x𝑥x, so we need to pose certain restrictions on ℑℑ\mathfrak{I}.

We argue that ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} should be bounded, with 111 marking the highest and 00 the lowest importance; that functions which are independent of a variable should rate these variables the lowest importance (e.g., ℑx​(f)=0subscriptℑ𝑥𝑓0\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f)=0 if f=y∨z𝑓𝑦𝑧f=y\lor z); that functions which depend on one variable only should rate these variables the highest importance (e.g., ℑx​(f)=1subscriptℑ𝑥𝑓1\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f)=1 for f=x𝑓𝑥f=x); that neither variable names nor polarities should play a role in determining their importance (e.g., ℑx​(x​z¯)=ℑz​(x​z¯)subscriptℑ𝑥𝑥¯𝑧subscriptℑ𝑧𝑥¯𝑧\mathfrak{I}_{x}(x\overline{z})=\mathfrak{I}_{z}(x\overline{z}), cf.  slepianNumberSymmetryTypes1953; golombClassificationBooleanFunctions1959):

(Bound), (Dum) for “dummy”, (Dic) for “dictator” and (Type) for “type invariance” were discussed above.(ModEC) (for “modular encapsulation consistency”) is the only property that allowsthe inference of non-trivial importance inequalities in different functions.Let us explain its intuition.We say that f𝑓f encapsulates hℎh on g𝑔g if these functions satisfy (i) and (ii) from (ModEC).Intuitively, together with (i), condition (ii) states that if one can control the output of g𝑔g, it is both easier to satisfy f𝑓f than hℎh (using fg/1≥hg/1subscript𝑓𝑔1subscriptℎ𝑔1f_{g{/}1}\geq h_{g{/}1}) and to falsify f𝑓f than hℎh (using hg/0≥fg/0subscriptℎ𝑔0subscript𝑓𝑔0h_{g{/}0}\geq f_{g{/}0}).We argue in (ModEC) that if f𝑓f encapsulates hℎh on g𝑔g, then g𝑔g’s impact on f𝑓f is higherthan on hℎh, and thus, the importance of variables in 𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔\mathtt{dep}(g) (cf. (iii)) should be also higher w.r.t. f𝑓f than w.r.t. hℎh.

{toappendix}The Winder preorder according to hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000 (Section 3 therein) is defined for a Boolean function f𝑓f and variables x,y𝑥𝑦x,y asx⩾fyifffx/1;y/0≥fx/0;y/1.formulae-sequencesubscript𝑓𝑥𝑦iffsubscript𝑓𝑥1𝑦0subscript𝑓𝑥0𝑦1x\geqslant_{f}y\quad\text{iff}\quad f_{x{/}1;y{/}0}\geq f_{x{/}0;y{/}1}.Similar to hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000 (Theorem 3.1 therein), we focus only on the case were f𝑓f is monotone in x𝑥x and y𝑦y. (ModEC) can then be seen as a generalization of this order in two senses: first, we abstract this order to the level of modules. Second, we abstract from an inequality between variables in one function to an inequality between variables in two functions. The following proposition now links the Winder preorder with (ModEC) and (Type):{apxproposition}Suppose ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} is an importance value function and let f𝑓f be a Boolean function monotone in x𝑥x and y𝑦y. Then:x⩾fy⟹ℑx​(f)≥ℑy​(f).subscript𝑓𝑥𝑦subscriptℑ𝑥𝑓subscriptℑ𝑦𝑓x\geqslant_{f}y\implies\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f)\geq\mathfrak{I}_{y}(f).

Together with (Type), (ModEC) implies the Winder preorder, which is similar in spirit(see hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000). However, (ModEC) generalizesto modular decompositions and allows inferring importance inequalities w.r.t. to differentfunctions.(See Section 3 in the appendix).

We say that an IVF is unbiased if ℑx​(g)=ℑx​(g¯)subscriptℑ𝑥𝑔subscriptℑ𝑥¯𝑔\mathfrak{I}_{x}(g)=\mathfrak{I}_{x}(\overline{g})holds for all Boolean functions g𝑔g and variables x𝑥x.That is, unbiased IVFs measure the impact of variables without any preference for one particularfunction outcome, while biased ones quantify the impact to enforce a function to return one orzero. Biased IVFs can, e.g., be useful when the task is to assign responsibility values for theviolation of a specification.

We defined IVFs following a conservative approach, collecting minimal requirements on IVFs.Further additional properties can improve on thepredictability and robustness of IVFs.

Rank preservation states that the relation between two variables should not change if the functionis embedded somewhere else.This can be desired, e.g., during a modeling process in which distinct Boolean functions are composed orfresh variables added, where rank preserving IVFs maintain the relative importance order of variables.We see this as a useful but optionalproperty of IVFs since an embedding couldchange some parameters of a function that might be relevant for the relationship of both variables.For example, if f=g​z𝑓𝑔𝑧f=gz with z∉𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝑧𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔z\not\in\mathtt{dep}(g), then the relative number of satisfying assignments is halved compared to g𝑔g. If x𝑥x is more importantthan y𝑦y in g𝑔g but highly relies on g𝑔g taking value one, it might be that this relationshipis reversed for f𝑓f (cf. example given in Section 4.1).

If an IVF is chain-rule decomposable, then the importance of a variable in a module is the product of (i) its importance w.r.t. the module and (ii) the importance of the module w.r.t. the function.Many values studied in this papersatisfy this property (Section 4).

Derivative dependence states that an IVF should quantify the changea variable induces on a Boolean function. It can be used to derive, e.g., the inequalityℑx1​(x1⊕x2​x3)≥ℑx1​(x2⊕x1​x3)subscriptℑsubscript𝑥1direct-sumsubscript𝑥1subscript𝑥2subscript𝑥3subscriptℑsubscript𝑥1direct-sumsubscript𝑥2subscript𝑥1subscript𝑥3\mathfrak{I}_{x_{1}}(x_{1}\oplus x_{2}x_{3})\geq\mathfrak{I}_{x_{1}}(x_{2}\oplus x_{1}x_{3}), which is not possible solely using(ModEC) since x1⊕x2​x3direct-sumsubscript𝑥1subscript𝑥2subscript𝑥3x_{1}\oplus x_{2}x_{3} is neither monotone in x1subscript𝑥1x_{1} nor in x2subscript𝑥2x_{2}. If a value function ℑℑ\mathfrak{I}(that is not necessarily an IVF) is derivative dependent, then this has some interesting implications.First, ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} is unbiased and satisfies (ModEC).Second, if ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} is weakly chain-rule decomposable (weakly rank preserving),then it is also chain-rule decomposable (rank preserving). Finally, if ℑℑ\mathfrak{I}satisfies (Dic) and (Dum), then it is also bounded by zero and one.As a consequence, if ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} is derivative dependent and satisfies (Dic),(Dum), and (Type), then ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} is an IVF.(See Section 3.1 in the appendix.)

In this section, we show that IVFs can be instantiated withseveral notions for importance values from the literature and thus providea unifying framework.

Using again the fact that ρ𝜌\rho is monotonically decreasing, we can conclude that (4.6) holds.

{toappendix}

{toappendix}Section 4 shows that some properties are satisfied only if one chooses a very specific share function. Proofs utilize Table 1, which contains scsscs\mathrm{scs}-values for six different functions. We combine implications of properties such as derivative dependence for these functions in order to derive statements about ρ𝜌\rho:

In full generality, the blame violates the optional properties for IVFs (see Section 3.1).For example, if ρ≠ρstep𝜌subscript𝜌step\rho\neq{\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}, then the ρ𝜌\rho-blameis neither chain-rule decomposable nor derivative dependent,and one can find counterexamples for the rank-preservation propertyfor ρfracsubscript𝜌frac{\rho_{\mathrm{frac}}} and ρexpsubscript𝜌exp{\rho_{\mathrm{exp}}}:

{apxpropositionrep}Let ρ𝜌\rho be a share function. Then the following statements are equivalent:(i)𝐁ρsuperscript𝐁𝜌{\bf{B}}^{\rho} is weakly chain-rule decomposable,(ii)𝐁ρsuperscript𝐁𝜌{\bf{B}}^{\rho} is derivative dependent, and(iii)ρ=ρstep𝜌subscript𝜌step\rho={\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}.Further, neither 𝐁ρfracsuperscript𝐁subscript𝜌frac{\bf{B}}^{\rho_{\mathrm{frac}}} nor 𝐁ρexpsuperscript𝐁subscript𝜌exp{\bf{B}}^{\rho_{\mathrm{exp}}} are weakly rank preserving.

For the equivalence of (i), (ii) and (iii), note that if ρ=ρstep𝜌subscript𝜌step\rho={\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}, then 𝐁ρsuperscript𝐁𝜌{\bf{B}}^{\rho} is simply the influence, which is both chain rule decomposable and derivative dependent as we will see later on (cf. Definition 9). Therefore, let us focus on the directions from (i) to (iii) and (ii) to (iii), i.e. we assume that 𝐁ρsuperscript𝐁𝜌{\bf{B}}^{\rho} is chain rule decomposable (resp. derivative dependent) and we want to show that this implies ρ=ρstep𝜌subscript𝜌step\rho={\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}.

For x3subscript𝑥3x_{3}, this is more frequently the case in g𝑔g than in f𝑓f (19%percent1919\% vs. 34%percent3434\% of all assignments).On the other hand, there is always a critical set for x0subscript𝑥0x_{0} in both f𝑓f and g𝑔g.Partly for this reason, the importance of x3subscript𝑥3x_{3} decreases more than x0subscript𝑥0x_{0}when switching from g𝑔g to f𝑓f.

{toappendix}

We modify the definition of critical setsin order to derive a modified blame thatsatisfies more optional properties for a wider class of share functions.

For a Boolean function f𝑓f, an assignment 𝒖𝒖{\bm{u}} over X𝑋X and a variable x𝑥x, the modified scsscs\mathrm{scs}is defined as the size mscsx𝒖​(f)subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f) of the smallest set S⊆X∖{x}𝑆𝑋𝑥S\subseteq X\setminus\{x\} that satisfiesf​(flipS​(𝒖))≠f​(flipS∪{x}​(𝒖)).𝑓subscriptflip𝑆𝒖𝑓subscriptflip𝑆𝑥𝒖f\big{(}\mathrm{flip}_{S}({\bm{u}})\big{)}\neq f\big{(}\mathrm{flip}_{S\cup\{x\}}({\bm{u}})\big{)}.If there is no such set, we set mscsx𝒖​(f)=∞subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)=\infty.

The definitions for responsibility and blame are analogous for the modified version, replacing scsscs\mathrm{scs} by mscsmscs\mathrm{mscs}. We denote by 𝐌𝐁ρsuperscript𝐌𝐁𝜌{\bf{MB}}^{\rho} the modified ρ𝜌\rho-blame,which is (in contrast to 𝐁ρsuperscript𝐁𝜌{\bf{B}}^{\rho}) always derivative dependent and evenchain-rule decomposable if ρ𝜌\rho is an exponential- or stepping-function:{toappendix}Like (4.5), there is a way to decompose the value mscsx𝒖​(f)superscriptsubscriptmscs𝑥𝒖𝑓\mathrm{mscs}_{x}^{\bm{u}}(f) if f𝑓f is modular in g𝑔g and x∈𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝑥𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔x\in\mathtt{dep}(g). However, we can express this decomposition using only mscsmscs\mathrm{mscs}:

{apxlemma}If f𝑓f is modular in g𝑔g, 𝒖𝒖{\bm{u}} an assignment over X𝑋X and x∈𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝑥𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔x\in\mathtt{dep}(g), thenmscsx𝒖​(f)=mscsx𝒖​(g)+mscsg𝒖​(f).subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑔subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑔𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)=\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(g)+\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{g}(f).

Suppose S𝑆S is an optimal solution to mscsx𝒖​(f)subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f), and define G=S∩𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g)𝐺𝑆𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔G=S\cap\mathtt{dep}(g) and F=S∩(X∖𝚍𝚎𝚙​(g))𝐹𝑆𝑋𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑔F=S\cap(X\setminus\mathtt{dep}(g)). By Section 2,Dx​f​(𝒖⊕𝟏S)=1⇒Dx​g​(𝒖⊕𝟏G)=Dg​f​(𝒖⊕𝟏F)=1,formulae-sequencesubscriptD𝑥𝑓direct-sum𝒖subscript1𝑆1⇒subscriptD𝑥𝑔direct-sum𝒖subscript1𝐺subscriptD𝑔𝑓direct-sum𝒖subscript1𝐹1\displaystyle\mathrm{D}_{x}f({\bm{u}}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{S})=1\quad\Rightarrow\quad\mathrm{D}_{x}g({\bm{u}}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{G})=\mathrm{D}_{g}f({\bm{u}}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{F})=1,so G𝐺G is a candidate solution to mscsx𝒖​(g)subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑔\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(g) and F𝐹F a candidate solution to mscsg𝒖​(f)subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑔𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{g}(f). The influence ben-orCollectiveCoinFlipping1985; kahnInfluenceVariablesBoolean1988; o2014analysis is a popular importance measure,defined as the probability that flipping the variable changes the function’s outcomefor uniformly distributed assignments:

It turns out that the influence is a special case of blame:{apxpropositionrep}𝐈=𝐌𝐁ρstep=𝐁ρstep.𝐈superscript𝐌𝐁subscript𝜌stepsuperscript𝐁subscript𝜌step{\bf{I}}={\bf{MB}}^{\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}={\bf{B}}^{\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}.{appendixproof}Follows from the fact that Dx​f​(𝒖)=ρstep​(mscsx𝒖​(f))=ρstep​(scsx𝒖​(f))subscriptD𝑥𝑓𝒖subscript𝜌stepsubscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓subscript𝜌stepsubscriptsuperscriptscs𝒖𝑥𝑓\mathrm{D}_{x}f({\bm{u}})={\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}(\mathrm{mscs}^{{\bm{u}}}_{x}(f))={\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}(\mathrm{scs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)) for all Boolean functions f𝑓f, variables x𝑥x and assignments 𝒖𝒖{\bm{u}}.

Since ρstep​(k)=0ksubscript𝜌step𝑘superscript0𝑘{\rho_{\mathrm{step}}}(k)=0^{k} for k≥1𝑘1k\geq 1, Definition 9 and Section 4show that the influence is a derivative-dependent, rank-preserving, and chain-rule decomposable IVF.

Call a value function ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} cofactor-additive iffor all Boolean functions f𝑓f and variables x≠z𝑥𝑧x\neq z:ℑx​(f)=1/2⋅ℑx​(fz/0)+1/2⋅ℑx​(fz/1).subscriptℑ𝑥𝑓⋅12subscriptℑ𝑥subscript𝑓𝑧0⋅12subscriptℑ𝑥subscript𝑓𝑧1\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f)=\nicefrac{{1}}{{2}}\cdot\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f_{z{/}0})+\nicefrac{{1}}{{2}}\cdot\mathfrak{I}_{x}(f_{z{/}1}).Using this notion, we axiomatically characterize the influence as follows.

{toappendix}The additional additivity property states that the importance of a variable to a function should always be the average importance with respect to the cofactors of another variable. We can applying this property repeatedly to obtain special cases which are covered by (Dum) and (Dic):

{apxtheoremrep}A value function ℑℑ\mathfrak{I} satisfies (Dic), (Dum), and cofactor-additivity if and only if ℑ=𝐈ℑ𝐈\mathfrak{I}={\bf{I}}.{appendixproof}Let us first establish that 𝐈𝐈{\bf{I}} indeed satisfies all of these properties. Moreover, we give a syntactic characterization of the influence by a comparisonto the two-sided Jeroslow-Wang heuristic used forSAT-solving jeroslowSolvingPropositionalSatisfiability1990; hookerBranchingRulesSatisfiability1995; marques-silvaImpactBranchingHeuristics1999. This value is defined for families of sets of literals, which are sets of subsets of X∪{z¯:z∈X}𝑋conditional-set¯𝑧𝑧𝑋X\cup\{\overline{z}:z\in X\}, and it weights subsets that contain x𝑥x or x¯¯𝑥\overline{x} by their respective lengths:

We call a set C𝐶C of literals trivial if there is a variable x𝑥x such that x∈C𝑥𝐶x\in Cand x¯∈C¯𝑥𝐶\overline{x}\in C. For a variable x𝑥x, say that 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} is x𝑥x-orthogonal if for all C,C′∈𝒟𝐶superscript𝐶′𝒟C,C^{\prime}\in\mathcal{D}, C≠C′𝐶superscript𝐶′C\neq C^{\prime}, there is a literal η∉{x,x¯}𝜂𝑥¯𝑥\eta\not\in\{x,\overline{x}\} such that η∈C𝜂𝐶\eta\in C and η¯∈C′¯𝜂superscript𝐶′\overline{\eta}\in C^{\prime}. Orthogonality is well-studied for DNFs HammerC2011. The two-sided Jeroslow-Wang value andthe influence agree up to a factor of two for some families of sets of literals when interpreting them as DNFs:

{toappendix}The following connection to the two-sided Jeroslow-Wang heuristic is established by counting the number of satisfying assignments. This heavily relies on the fact that every pair of clauses is distinguished by a literal that occurs negatively in one and positively in the other:

Note that we caninterpret 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} as a CNF as well, since the influence does not distinguish betweena function and its dual (Section 3.2).Note that every Boolean function can be expressed by a family 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} that satisfies the conditions of Section 4.2.1. For this, we construct the canonical DNF corresponding to f𝑓f andresolve all monomials that differ only in x𝑥x.(See Definition 10 in the appendix.)

{toappendix}The following proposition shows that every Boolean function f𝑓f can be represented by DNF made up of a family of sets of literals 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} that satisfies the premise in Section 4.2.1. Say that a family of sets of literals 𝒜𝒜\mathcal{A} is a DNF representation of f𝑓f if f=⋁C∈𝒜⋀η∈Cη𝑓subscript𝐶𝒜subscript𝜂𝐶𝜂f=\bigvee_{C\in\mathcal{A}}\bigwedge_{\eta\in C}\eta. Then:{apxproposition}Suppose f𝑓f is a Boolean function and x𝑥x a variable. Then there exists a family of sets of literals 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} such that (i) 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} is a DNF representation of f𝑓f, (ii) all C∈𝒟𝐶𝒟C\in\mathcal{D} are non-trivial and (iii) 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} is x𝑥x-orthogonal.

Let us first define ℛℛ\mathcal{R} as the family of sets of literals that corresponds to the canonical DNF of f𝑓f, i.e.ℛ={{z:𝒖​(z)=1}∪{z¯:𝒖​(z)=0}:𝒖∈{0,1}X,f​(𝒖)=1}.ℛconditional-setconditional-set𝑧𝒖𝑧1conditional-set¯𝑧𝒖𝑧0formulae-sequence𝒖superscript01𝑋𝑓𝒖1\displaystyle\mathcal{R}=\{\{z:{\bm{u}}(z)=1\}\cup\{\overline{z}:{\bm{u}}(z)=0\}:{\bm{u}}\in\{0,1\}^{X},f({\bm{u}})=1\}.We now resolve all sets of literals C𝐶C for which C∪{x}∈ℛ𝐶𝑥ℛC\cup\{x\}\in\mathcal{R} and C∪{x¯}∈ℛ𝐶¯𝑥ℛC\cup\{\overline{x}\}\in\mathcal{R} holds:𝒟={C∈ℛ:x,x¯∉C}∪{C∖{x,x¯}:C∪{x},C∪{x¯}∈ℛ}.𝒟conditional-set𝐶ℛ𝑥¯𝑥𝐶conditional-set𝐶𝑥¯𝑥𝐶𝑥𝐶¯𝑥ℛ\displaystyle\mathcal{D}=\{C\in\mathcal{R}:x,\overline{x}\not\in C\}\cup\{C\setminus\{x,\overline{x}\}:C\cup\{x\},C\cup\{\overline{x}\}\in\mathcal{R}\}.For example, if f=y∧(x∨z)𝑓𝑦𝑥𝑧f=y\land(x\lor z), then ℛ={{y,x,z¯},{y,x,z},{y,x¯,z}}ℛ𝑦𝑥¯𝑧𝑦𝑥𝑧𝑦¯𝑥𝑧\mathcal{R}=\{\{y,x,\overline{z}\},\{y,x,z\},\{y,\overline{x},z\}\} and 𝒟={{y,x,z¯},{y,z}}𝒟𝑦𝑥¯𝑧𝑦𝑧\mathcal{D}=\{\{y,x,\overline{z}\},\{y,z\}\}.

If 𝒜𝒜\mathcal{A} is a DNF representation of f𝑓f and C𝐶C a set of literals such that C∪{x},C∪{x¯}∈𝒜𝐶𝑥𝐶¯𝑥𝒜C\cup\{x\},C\cup\{\overline{x}\}\in\mathcal{A}, then note that both C∪{x}𝐶𝑥C\cup\{x\} and C∪{x¯}𝐶¯𝑥C\cup\{\overline{x}\} can be resolved to C∖{x,x¯}𝐶𝑥¯𝑥C\setminus\{x,\overline{x}\} without changing the fact that the resulting family is a DNF representation of f𝑓f. More precisely, (𝒜∖{C∪{x},C∪{x¯}})∪(C∖{x,x¯})𝒜𝐶𝑥𝐶¯𝑥𝐶𝑥¯𝑥(\mathcal{A}\setminus\{C\cup\{x\},C\cup\{\overline{x}\}\})\cup(C\setminus\{x,\overline{x}\}) must also be a DNF representation of f𝑓f. Repeatedly applying this argument for all C𝐶C such that C∪{x},C∪{x¯}∈ℛ𝐶𝑥𝐶¯𝑥ℛC\cup\{x\},C\cup\{\overline{x}\}\in\mathcal{R} shows that 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} must indeed be a DNF representation of f𝑓f, i.e. satisfy (i).For (ii), note that there is no C∈ℛ𝐶ℛC\in\mathcal{R} that is trivial. Since every set in 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} is also a subset of a set in ℛℛ\mathcal{R}, no element of 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} can be trivial.Finally, for (iii), note that ℛℛ\mathcal{R} is orthogonal, i.e. for all C,C′∈ℛ𝐶superscript𝐶′ℛC,C^{\prime}\in\mathcal{R}, C≠C′𝐶superscript𝐶′C\neq C^{\prime} there exists a literal η𝜂\eta such that η∈C𝜂𝐶\eta\in C and η¯∈C′¯𝜂superscript𝐶′\overline{\eta}\in C^{\prime}. After resolving all sets that differ only in η∈{x,x¯}𝜂𝑥¯𝑥\eta\in\{x,\overline{x}\}, all resulting pairs of sets must differ in at least one literal not in {x,x¯}𝑥¯𝑥\{x,\overline{x}\}. Thus, 𝒟𝒟\mathcal{D} must be x𝑥x-orthogonal.∎

{toappendix}

Attribution schemes analogous to what we call value functions were already studied in thecontext of game theory, most often with emphasis on Shapley- and Banzhafvalues shapleyValueNPersonGames1953; banzhafWeightedVotingDoesn1965.They are studied w.r.t. cooperative games, which are a popular way of modeling collaborative behavior.Instead of Boolean assignments, their domains are subsets (coalitions) of X𝑋X.Specifically, cooperative games are of the form v:2X→ℝ:𝑣→superscript2𝑋ℝv\colon 2^{X}\rightarrow\mathbb{R}, in which the value v​(S)𝑣𝑆v(S) is associatedwith the payoff that variables (players) in S𝑆S receive when collaborating. Since more cooperation generally means higher payoffs, they are often assumed to be monotonically increasing w.r.t. set inclusion.In its unconstrained form, they are essentially pseudo Boolean functions.

We denote by 𝔾​(X)𝔾𝑋\mathbb{G}(X) the set of all cooperative games.If 𝚒𝚖𝚊𝚐𝚎​(v)⊆{0,1}𝚒𝚖𝚊𝚐𝚎𝑣01\mathtt{image}(v)\subseteq\{0,1\}, then we call v𝑣v simple.For a cooperative game v𝑣v, we denote by ∂xvsubscript𝑥𝑣\partial_{x}v the cooperative game that computes the“derivative” of v𝑣v w.r.t. x𝑥x, which is ∂xv​(S)=v​(S∪{x})−v​(S∖{x})subscript𝑥𝑣𝑆𝑣𝑆𝑥𝑣𝑆𝑥\partial_{x}v(S)=v(S\cup\{x\})-v(S\setminus\{x\}). We compose cooperative games using operations such as ⋅,+,−,∧,∨⋅\cdot,+,-,\land,\lor etc., where (v∘w)​(S)=v​(S)∘w​(S)𝑣𝑤𝑆𝑣𝑆𝑤𝑆(v\circ w)(S)=v(S)\circ w(S).For ∼∈{≥,≤,=}\mathbin{\sim}\in\{\geq,\leq,=\}, we also write v∼wsimilar-to𝑣𝑤v\sim w if v​(S)∼w​(S)similar-to𝑣𝑆𝑤𝑆v(S)\sim w(S) for all S⊆X𝑆𝑋S\subseteq X.The set of variables v𝑣v dependson is defined as 𝚍𝚎𝚙​(v)={x∈X:∂xv≠0}𝚍𝚎𝚙𝑣conditional-set𝑥𝑋subscript𝑥𝑣0\mathtt{dep}(v)=\{x\in X:\partial_{x}v\neq 0\}.

Cooperative game mappings map Boolean functions to cooperative games.Specific instances of such mappings have previously beeninvestigated by hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000; biswasInfluenceSetVariables2022.We provide a general definition of this concept toshow how it can be used to construct IVFs.

An example is the characteristic CGM ζ𝜁\zeta given by ζf​(S)=f​(𝟏S)subscript𝜁𝑓𝑆𝑓subscript1𝑆\zeta_{f}(S)=f(\mathbf{1}_{S}), where 𝟏S​(x)=1subscript1𝑆𝑥1\mathbf{1}_{S}(x)=1 iff x∈S𝑥𝑆x\in S.We study various importance-inducing CGMs in the following sections.Note that ζ𝜁\zeta is not importance inducing: for example, it violates (BoundCG) since ∂xζf​(∅)=−1subscript𝑥subscript𝜁𝑓1\partial_{x}\zeta_{f}(\varnothing)=-1 for f=x¯𝑓¯𝑥f=\overline{x}.

In the following sections, we study two noveland the already-known CGM ofhammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000.By Section 4 we can focus on theirproperties as CGMs, knowing that any composition with the Shapley value or other expectations of contributions will induce IVFs.

{toappendix}

The first CGM interprets the “power” of a coalition as its ability to force a function’s outcometo one:If there is an assignment for a set of variables that yields outcome one no matter the valuesof other variables, we assign this set a value of one, and zero otherwise.

One can think of a dual notion of the CGM ω𝜔\omega that reverses the order of both quantifiers.Intuitively, we are now allowed tochoose an assignment depending on the values of the remaining variables:

If we compose ν𝜈\nu with an expectation of contributions thatsatisfies c​(k)=c​(n−1−k)𝑐𝑘𝑐𝑛1𝑘c(k)=c(n{-}1{-}k) for all k∈{0,…,n−1}𝑘0…𝑛1k\in\{0,\dots,n{-}1\},which is a condition satisfied both by the Shapley and Banzhaf values,the induced importance of a variable equals its importance w.r.t. ω𝜔\omega and the negated function:

{toappendix}

Hammer, Kogan and Rothblum hammerEvaluationStrengthRelevance2000 (HKR)defined a CGM that measures the power of variables by how constant they make a function if assigned random values.It depends on the following notion of constancy measure:

The following functions are instances of constancy measures:•κquad​(a)=4​(a−1/2)2subscript𝜅quad𝑎4superscript𝑎122{\kappa_{\mathrm{quad}}}(a)=4(a-\nicefrac{{1}}{{2}})^{2},•κlog​(a)=1+a​lb​(a)+(1−a)​lb​(1−a)subscript𝜅log𝑎1𝑎lb𝑎1𝑎lb1𝑎{\kappa_{\mathrm{log}}}(a)=1+a\mathrm{lb}(a)+(1{-}a)\mathrm{lb}(1{-}a) with 0​l​b​(0)=00lb000\mathrm{lb}(0)=0,•κabs​(a)=2​|a−1/2|subscript𝜅abs𝑎2𝑎12{\kappa_{\mathrm{abs}}}(a)=2|a-\nicefrac{{1}}{{2}}|.For a constancy measure κ𝜅\kappa and a Boolean function f𝑓f, the κ𝜅\kappa-constancy of f𝑓f is the value κ​(𝔼​[f])𝜅𝔼delimited-[]𝑓\kappa(\mathbb{E}[f]), which measures how balanced the share of ones and zeros is.It is close to one if f𝑓f is very unbalanced and close to zero if the share of zeros and ones in f𝑓f is (almost) the same.The power of a set of variables S𝑆S is now measured in terms of the expected κ𝜅\kappa-constancy of f𝑓f if variables in S𝑆S are fixed to random values:

Section 4 shows that HκquadsuperscriptHsubscript𝜅quad\mathrm{H}^{\kappa_{\mathrm{quad}}} is a chain-ruledecomposable and importance-inducing CGM.It is open whether other constancy measuresare importance inducing too.

In this section, we present and evaluate computation schemes for blame, influence, and CGMs.While there exists a practical approach based on modelcounting for the influence in CNFs traxler2009variable, we are only aware of naïve computations ofCHK’s blame dubslaff2022causality.Details are given in the appendix.

We focus on the modified blame. CHK’s blame can be computed in a very similar fashion.Observe that for a Boolean function f𝑓f and x∈X𝑥𝑋x\in X,𝐌𝐁xρ​(f)=𝔼​[γ0]+∑k=1n−1ρ​(k)​(𝔼​[γk]−𝔼​[γk−1]),subscriptsuperscript𝐌𝐁𝜌𝑥𝑓𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝛾0superscriptsubscript𝑘1𝑛1𝜌𝑘𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝛾𝑘𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝛾𝑘1{\bf{MB}}^{\rho}_{x}(f)=\mathbb{E}[\gamma_{0}]+\textstyle\sum_{k=1}^{n{-}1}\rho(k)(\mathbb{E}[\gamma_{k}]-\mathbb{E}[\gamma_{k{-}1}]),where γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} is the Boolean function for which γk​(𝒖)=1subscript𝛾𝑘𝒖1\gamma_{k}({\bm{u}})=1 iff mscsx𝒖​(f)≤ksubscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓𝑘\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)\leq k.We devise two approaches for computing 𝔼​[γk]𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝛾𝑘\mathbb{E}[\gamma_{k}].The first represents γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} through BDDs using the following recursion scheme: mscsx𝒖​(f)≤ksubscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓𝑘\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)\leq k holds iff•k=0𝑘0k=0 and f​(𝒖)≠f​(flip{x}​(𝒖))𝑓𝒖𝑓subscriptflip𝑥𝒖f({\bm{u}})\neq f(\mathrm{flip}_{\{x\}}({\bm{u}})), or•k>0𝑘0k>0 and–mscsx𝒖​(f)≤k−1subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓𝑘1\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)\leq k{-}1 or–there is y≠x𝑦𝑥y\neq x such that mscsx𝒖​(f​[y/y¯])≤k−1subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓delimited-[]𝑦¯𝑦𝑘1\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f[y/\overline{y}])\leq k{-}1.This allows us to construct BDDs for γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} from γk−1subscript𝛾𝑘1\gamma_{k{-}1}, which lends itself to BDD-based approaches since γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} does not necessarily increase in size as k𝑘k grows.The second approach introduces new existentially quantified variables in the input formula of f𝑓f to model occurrences of variables in critical sets of mscsx𝒖​(f)subscriptsuperscriptmscs𝒖𝑥𝑓\mathrm{mscs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f).With an additional cardinality constraint restricting the number of variables in critical sets to at most k𝑘k,we can use projected model counting to compute 𝔼​[γk]𝔼delimited-[]subscript𝛾𝑘\mathbb{E}[\gamma_{k}].

θf,x,c,k={f∧f​[x/x¯]¯k=0,c=1f¯∧f​[x/x¯]k=0,c=0θf,x,c,k−1∨⋁y∈X∖{x}θf,x,c,k−1​[y/y¯]k>0subscript𝜃𝑓𝑥𝑐𝑘cases𝑓¯𝑓delimited-[]𝑥¯𝑥formulae-sequence𝑘0𝑐1¯𝑓𝑓delimited-[]𝑥¯𝑥formulae-sequence𝑘0𝑐0subscript𝜃𝑓𝑥𝑐𝑘1subscript𝑦𝑋𝑥subscript𝜃𝑓𝑥𝑐𝑘1delimited-[]𝑦¯𝑦𝑘0\displaystyle\theta_{f,x,c,k}=\begin{cases}f\land\overline{f[x/\overline{x}]}&k=0,c=1\\\overline{f}\land f[x/\overline{x}]&k=0,c=0\\\theta_{f,x,c,k{-}1}\lor\bigvee_{y\in X\setminus\{x\}}\theta_{f,x,c,k{-}1}[y/\overline{y}]&k>0\end{cases}Note that for BDDs, flipping variables and complementing and composing them once(!) using ∧,∨\land,\lor, etc. increases BDD sizes only polynomially and sometimes even decreases them. The last fact is especially important for k>0𝑘0k>0, as it is often times the case that the BDD’s size decreases as k𝑘k grows, which is due to the fact that θf,x,c,ksubscript𝜃𝑓𝑥𝑐𝑘\theta_{f,x,c,k} becomes more and more satisfiable. Other approaches, such as the representation of θf,x,c,ksubscript𝜃𝑓𝑥𝑐𝑘\theta_{f,x,c,k} by propositional formulas, do not have this advantage and will always “explode”. Let ϕitalic-ϕ\phi be a propositional formula that represents f𝑓f and suppose x∈𝚟𝚊𝚛​(ϕ)=Φ𝑥𝚟𝚊𝚛italic-ϕΦx\in\mathtt{var}(\phi)=\Phi occurs in ϕitalic-ϕ\phi. The basis for this approach lays in the observation that we can devise a sequence of “short” propositional formulas {χk}k=0n−1superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝜒𝑘𝑘0𝑛1\{\chi_{k}\}_{k=0}^{n{-}1} with some extra variables N⊆X∖Φ𝑁𝑋ΦN\subseteq X\setminus\Phi such that for all assignments 𝒖𝒖{\bm{u}} over ΦΦ\Phi and k∈{0,…,n−1}𝑘0…𝑛1k\in\{0,\dots,n{-}1\},∃𝒗∈{0,1}N.χk​(𝒖;𝒗)iffscsx𝒖​(f)≤k.formulae-sequence𝒗superscript01𝑁subscript𝜒𝑘𝒖𝒗iffsubscriptsuperscriptscs𝒖𝑥𝑓𝑘\displaystyle\exists{\bm{v}}\in\{0,1\}^{N}.\chi_{k}({\bm{u}};{\bm{v}})\quad\text{iff}\quad\mathrm{scs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)\leq k.Counting the number of 𝒖𝒖{\bm{u}}’s that satisfy the left hand side is done through projected model counting. The general idea is that these new variables contained in N𝑁N certify the existence of a candidate solution S𝑆S of size k𝑘k or less for the right side. The definition of χksubscript𝜒𝑘\chi_{k} contains roughly two parts: one that states that S𝑆S cannot have more than k𝑘k elements, and one that makes sure that S𝑆S is indeed a candidate solution, i.e. satisfies ϕ​(𝒖)=ϕ​(𝒖⊕𝟏S)≠ϕ​(𝒖⊕𝟏S⊕𝟏x)italic-ϕ𝒖italic-ϕdirect-sum𝒖subscript1𝑆italic-ϕdirect-sum𝒖subscript1𝑆subscript1𝑥\phi({\bm{u}})=\phi({\bm{u}}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{S})\neq\phi({\bm{u}}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{S}{\oplus}\mathbf{1}_{x}). The former part is ensured by using a cardinality constraint and the latter part by introducing a set of new variables that model the action of adding elements to S𝑆S:

First, introduce |Φ|−1Φ1|\Phi|-1 new variables by setting V={vy:y∈Φ∖{x}}⊆X∖Φ𝑉conditional-setsubscript𝑣𝑦𝑦Φ𝑥𝑋ΦV=\{v_{y}:y\in\Phi\setminus\{x\}\}\subseteq X\setminus\Phi. These variables are used in the replacementψ=ϕ[y/(y⊕vy):vy∈V],\psi=\phi[y/(y\oplus v_{y}):v_{y}\in V],which is easy to carry out on formulas and doubles ϕitalic-ϕ\phi’s size in the worst case. Furthermore, we use the totalizer encoding bailleuxEfficientCNFEncoding2003 to devise a formula atmostksubscriptatmost𝑘\texttt{atmost}_{k} that introduces O​(n​log⁡n)𝑂𝑛𝑛O(n\log n) new variables denoted by the set W𝑊W such that 𝚟𝚊𝚛​(atmostk)=W∪V𝚟𝚊𝚛subscriptatmost𝑘𝑊𝑉\mathtt{var}(\texttt{atmost}_{k})=W\cup V and∃𝒘∈{0,1}W.atmostk​(𝒗;𝒘)iff|𝒗|≤k.formulae-sequence𝒘superscript01𝑊subscriptatmost𝑘𝒗𝒘iff𝒗𝑘\displaystyle\exists{\bm{w}}\in\{0,1\}^{W}.\;\texttt{atmost}_{k}({\bm{v}};{\bm{w}})\quad\text{iff}\quad|{\bm{v}}|\leq k.for all assignments 𝒗𝒗{\bm{v}} over V𝑉V. Finally, for (5.5), we simply apply the definition of scsscs\mathrm{scs}.

∎

In case f𝑓f is given as a CNF formula, we use Traxler’s method to compute the influence traxler2009variable.For all other formulas, note thatstandard satisfiability-preserving transformations do not preserve influence values:For example, applying the Tseytin transformation tox∨x¯​y𝑥¯𝑥𝑦x{\lor}\overline{x}y results in a CNF where x𝑥x has a higher influence than y𝑦y.

However, the influence is proportional to the number of models of Dx​fsubscriptD𝑥𝑓\mathrm{D}_{x}f.If f𝑓f is given by a BDD, computing a representation of Dx​fsubscriptD𝑥𝑓\mathrm{D}_{x}f means squaring f𝑓f’s size in the worst case,while the formula-based representation only doubles it.For the latter case, we can count the models of Dx​fsubscriptD𝑥𝑓\mathrm{D}_{x}f using a Tseytin transformationand a standard model counter.

The dominating CGM computes a simple game, which is essentially aBoolean function, and therefore permits a representation by BDDs. Moreover,using a BDD representation of f𝑓f, we compute ωfsubscript𝜔𝑓\omega_{f}using a recursion on cofactors of variables z𝑧z,(ωf)z/1=ωfz/1∨ωfz/0subscriptsubscript𝜔𝑓𝑧1subscript𝜔subscript𝑓𝑧1subscript𝜔subscript𝑓𝑧0(\omega_{f})_{z{/}1}=\omega_{f_{z{/}1}}\!\!\lor\omega_{f_{z{/}0}}  and  (ωf)z/0=ωfz/0∧fz/1subscriptsubscript𝜔𝑓𝑧0subscript𝜔subscript𝑓𝑧0subscript𝑓𝑧1(\omega_{f})_{z{/}0}=\omega_{f_{z{/}0}\land f_{z{/}1}}.The Banzhaf value of x𝑥x in ωfsubscript𝜔𝑓\omega_{f} is then just𝔼​[(ωf)x/1]−𝔼​[(ωf)x/0],𝔼delimited-[]subscriptsubscript𝜔𝑓𝑥1𝔼delimited-[]subscriptsubscript𝜔𝑓𝑥0\mathbb{E}[(\omega_{f})_{x{/}1}]-\mathbb{E}[(\omega_{f})_{x{/}0}],which poses no effort once the BDD of ωfsubscript𝜔𝑓\omega_{f} is constructed. The rectifying CGM can be computed analogously.

We have implemented Traxler’s methodand our new computation schemesin Python, using BuDDy lind-nielsenBuDDyBinaryDecision1999 as BDD backend with automatic reordering and GPMC suzukiExtensionDPLLBasedModelCounting2015; suzukiImprovementProjectedModelCounting2017 for (projected) model counting.To evaluate our approaches, we conducted experiments on Boolean functions given as CNFs thatwere either randomly generated or generated from the ISCAS’99 dataset davidson1999itc; compileProject.We always computed importance values w.r.t. the first variable in the input CNFand averaged the timings over 20 runs each.Our experiments were carried out on a Linux system with an i5-10400F CPU at 2.90GHz and 16GB of RAM.To compare our BDD-based and model counting approaches, Figure 1 shows timings forblame computations on random CNFs. Here, the BDD-based approach clearly outperformsthe one based on projected model counting. This is also reflected in real-world benchmarks from ISCAS’99 shownin Table 2, where the approach based on model counting runs into timeoutsfor even small instances.Table 2 shows that computations for influence values based on model countingscale better than the BDD-based approach, mainly due to an expensive initial BDD construction.Computing the BDD of the dominating CGM is done without much overhead once the BDD for the CNF is given.

The proposed algorithms were tested on two datasets of randomly generated CNFs and the ISCAS’99 benchmark. The former two are specified by tuples (n,m,k)𝑛𝑚𝑘(n,m,k) where n𝑛n is the number of variables, m𝑚m the number of clauses and k𝑘k the width of every clause.The first dataset contains random (n,3​n,7)𝑛3𝑛7(n,3n,7)-CNFs which have expected values from at least 0.30.30.3 up to 0.90.90.9. These CNFs are very balanced; the probability that a random assignment satisfies a random CNF from this dataset is 0.540.540.54. The second dataset consists of random (n,7​n,4)𝑛7𝑛4(n,7n,4)-CNFs which have expected values from 7⋅10−14⋅7superscript10147\cdot 10^{-14} up to 0.01950.01950.0195. Only small amounts of assignments satisfy CNFs of this dataset. For each n𝑛n, we have sampled 101010 CNFs and computed the mean running time of every algorithm. This includes parsing and constructing the BDDs with fixed but essentially random variable orders. The (modified) blame is computed for ρ=ρfrac𝜌subscript𝜌frac\rho={\rho_{\mathrm{frac}}}. Blame and influence values are computed with respect to the first variable.

Let us compare the results of both datasets as shown in Figure 2:•Figure 2(a): For formula based approaches, computing the influence includes parsing the CNF as a formula ϕitalic-ϕ\phi, applying the transformation ϕ↦ϕx/0⊕ϕx/1maps-toitalic-ϕdirect-sumsubscriptitalic-ϕ𝑥0subscriptitalic-ϕ𝑥1\phi\mapsto\phi_{x{/}0}\oplus\phi_{x{/}1} and then re-translating the result back into a CNF via a Tseytin transformation. This increases the original size only linearly, which is arguably the reason for why GPMC’s performance is close (or becomes close over time) to the approach of traxler2009variable for both datasets. Our BDD-based implementation is faster for smaller numbers of variables, but is overtaken by GPMC eventually. As one can see on the right side, GPMC is much faster for random CNFs with low expected values. Note the influence is computed with little to no overhead once the BDD construction is done.•Figure 2(b): Recall that we have presented two possible ways of computing the (modified) blame. In our case, the projected model counting approach via GPMC performs worse than the BDD-based approach. Note that the modified blame is computed slightly faster than the blame in both cases. However, the computation is still limited to small amounts of variables.•Figure 2(c): Our proposed approach for the representation of the dominating cooperative game mapping through BDDs performs best in the second dataset. This is because most of these CNFs have a very low share of satisfied assignments, so one expects the BDDs to have small sizes, which in turn allows one to compute the dominating cooperative game mapping efficiently. (Only few subsets S⊆X𝑆𝑋S\subseteq X satisfy ωf​(S)=1subscript𝜔𝑓𝑆1\omega_{f}(S)=1.) Thus, the bottle neck for sparsely satisfied CNFs seems to be the BDD’s construction time.

We conclude that (i) the influence can be computed efficiently using modern model counting software, (ii) the blame can be computed efficiently for smaller amounts of variables using a BDD-based approach and (iii) given a BDD representation of a Boolean function, the dominating cooperative game mapping can usually be computed efficiently. For both influence and dominating cooperative game mapping, the BDD construction time is the biggest bottle neck.

Finally, let us consider the ISCAS’99 benchmark compileProject. Table 3 shows how well our proposed approaches perform on average for 20 runs. The smallest instances of this benchmark still permit the computation of blame values, which was done for the instances b02, b06 and b01. When plotting the number of nodes in the BDDs representing the functions {γk}k=0nsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝛾𝑘𝑘0𝑛\{\gamma_{k}\}_{k=0}^{n} (recall that for a fixed Boolean function f𝑓f and variable x𝑥x, γk​(𝒖)=1subscript𝛾𝑘𝒖1\gamma_{k}({\bm{u}})=1 if and only if scsx𝒖​(f)≤ksubscriptsuperscriptscs𝒖𝑥𝑓𝑘\mathrm{scs}^{\bm{u}}_{x}(f)\leq k), we obtain Figure 3. Observe that as k𝑘k grows, γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} becomes more and more satisfiable. Consequently, we can see that the sizes of BDDs representing γksubscript𝛾𝑘\gamma_{k} first increase but later on decrease until they hit zero nodes (i.e. are one everywhere).

This paper introduced IVFs as a way to formally reason about importance of variables in Boolean functions.We established general statements about IVFs, also providing insights on notions of importancefrom the literature by showing that they all belong to the class of IVFs.Apart from revealing several relations between known IVFs, we have shown howto generate new ones inspired by cooperative game theory.

For future work, we will study properties with strict importance inequalities,IVFs for sets of variables, IVFs for pseudo Boolean functions,and global values similar to the total influence o2014analysis.On the empirical side, the generation of splitting rules for SAT-solvers andvariable-order heuristics for BDDs based on differentinstances of IVFs are promising avenues to pursue.
